Evans v. West Norriton Tp. Municipal Authority


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


370 Pa. 150; 87 A.2d 474; 1952 Pa. LEXIS 324


Argued January 16, 1952  
March 24, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 70, Jan. T., 1952, from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Feb. T., 1951, in Equity, No. 11, in case of Ida T. Evans v. West Norriton Township Municipal Authority. Decree affirmed.

Bill in equity.

Defendant's preliminary objections sustained and order entered dismissing bill, opinion by FORREST, J. Plaintiff appealed.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed; each party to bear her or its respective costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed a decree of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed his bill in equity filed against defendant township municipal authority. The taxpayer sought to enjoin the municipal authority from imposing and collecting sewer charges assessed against her property on the grounds that § 4B(s) of the Municipality Authorities Act (Act), 53 P.S. 2900z-5, violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 20.


OVERVIEW: The township created the municipal authority, a quasi-public governmental body, pursuant to the Act to build a sewer. The taxpayer sought to enjoin the municipal authority from imposing sewer charges against her land alleging that § 4(b) of the Act violated Pa. Const. art. III, § 20. Section 20 prohibited the general assembly from delegating the authority to levy taxes to any special commission or private corporation. The trial court dismissed the taxpayer's action. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that 53 P.S. 2900z-5: (1) did not delegate the taxing power or the performance of municipal functions to an authority created thereunder; (2) that the plan for the sewer, the cost of construction, and the liening thereof determined initially by an authority was valid only if and when approved by the elected commissioners; and (3) that the imposition of charges and the liening thereof by the elected public officials in the mode prescribed by the Act did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 20.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree of the trial court that dismissed the taxpayer's bill in equity filed against the municipal authority.

 

471 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Appeal of School Dist.


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


370 Pa. 161; 87 A.2d 480; 1952 Pa. LEXIS 325


Argued January 10, 1952  
March 24, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 35, Jan. T., 1952, from order of Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County, Miscellaneous Docket C, page 124, in re Appeal from Mercantile Tax Levy by the School District of the City of Allentown. Order reversed.

Appeals from mercantile tax resolution of School District. Before HENNINGER, P.J. and DIEFENDERFER, J.

Order entered sustaining tax. Objectors appealed.



DISPOSITION: The order of the court below is reversed; costs to be paid by appellee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors sought review of the decision of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which held that the resolution of the School District of the City of Allentown (Act), levying a mercantile tax on wholesale and retail goods, wares, and merchandise was valid.


OVERVIEW: The Act authorized political subdivisions, including school districts of the second class, to tax anything not specifically excepted except for the dollar volume of resale business. The Act had different tax computations depending on how long someone had been in business. The court reversed the trial court's decision and determined that the Act was invalid. The different tax computation formulae were arbitrary and had no reasonable or practical justification; they created a substantial and unjustifiable inequality of tax burden; and they established an estimated or theoretical basis for tax that was different from actual gross income that was the standard prescribed by the plain language of the Act. Uniformity required substantial equality of tax burden. The imposition of taxes which were to a substantial degree unequal in their operation or effect upon similar kinds of business or property, or upon persons in the same classification, was prohibited. Where a formula or method of computing a tax would, in its operation or effect, produce arbitrary or unjust or unreasonably discriminatory results, the constitutional provision relating to uniformity was violated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision and determined that the Act was invalid.

United States Steel Co. v. County of Allegheny


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


369 Pa. 423; 86 A.2d 838; 1952 Pa. LEXIS 283


Argued October 3, 1951  
January 7, 1952 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 175 and 176, March T., 1951, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, July T., 1951, No. 3481, in case of United States Steel Company v. The County of Allegheny and Charles F. Dinan, County Treasurer. Order affirmed; reargument refused March 25, 1952.

Proceeding upon petition of plaintiff and rule to show cause why real estate taxes should not be paid into court rather than to county treasurer.

Rule made absolute and order entered directing undisputed portion of taxes paid into court to be paid over to treasurer and balance retained in hands of court, before McNAUGHER, P.J., MARSHALL and KENNEDY, JJ., opinion per curiam. Defendants appealed.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (New York) that directed the undisputed portion of taxes paid into court to be paid over to the county treasurer and the balance retained in the hands of the court pending trial in favor of appellee steel company.


OVERVIEW: The company presented a petition to the lower court asking leave to pay into court the full amount of the county real estate taxes which were assessed against it for a certain year under and in accordance with the provisions of the Act of July 15, 1935, 72 P.S. § 5020-518. The county treasurer opposed the payment of the company's taxes into court on the ground that the privilege to do so was expressly abolished by the Act of July 12, 1935, 72 P.S. § 5020-518. The lower court sustained the company's petition and directed the prothonotary to pay to the county treasurer a certain sum and to retain the balance of taxes at issue pending the trial and determination of the company's assessment appeals. The county appealed and the court affirmed the order of the lower court. The court determined that the two acts were absolutely irreconcilable as the Act of July 12 expressly abolished payment of disputed taxes into court; while the Act of July 15 expressly authorized payment of disputed taxes into court. On that point the two acts were inconsistent, conflicting, and clearly irreconcilable. The court held that the last act prevailed and the earlier act was repealed by necessary implication.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court in favor of the company and against the county that directed the company to pay a portion of the undisputed taxes that had been paid into court over to the county treasurer and to have the balance retained in the hands of the court pending trial.

Federal Deposit Ins. Corp. v. Board of Finance & Revenue


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 463; 84 A.2d 495; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 493


Argued September 28, 1951  
November 15, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 10 to 14, inclusive, May T., 1952, from decrees of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, 1949, Commonwealth Docket Nos. 235, 247, 248, 249 and 274, respectively, in cases of Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation v. Board of Finance & Revenue, etc., The First National Bank of Susquehanna v. Same and Nazareth National Bank & Trust Company v. Same. Decrees affirmed.

Mandamus for refund of taxes erroneously paid.

Defendant's preliminary objections sustained and order entered dismissing complaint, opinion by RICHARDS, P.J. Plaintiffs appealed.



DISPOSITION: The decrees of the court below dismissing the actions in mandamus are affirmed at the cost of the respective appellants therein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged decrees from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed the taxpayers' complaint in mandamus seeking a refund of taxes that had been paid to respondent board of finance and revenue.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer, an insurance company, filed a petition with the board for a refund of a part of the tax imposed upon the shares of the bank which, it asserted, had been erroneously assessed because the fiscal officers of the state had employed the book value of the bank's assets instead of their actual value and had included shares held by religious, charitable and educational institutions. The board refused the petition and the taxpayer filed an action in mandamus seeking the refund. The action in mandamus was dismissed and the taxpayer appealed from the decision. The court stated that to be to entitled to obtain a refund, the petition had to be filed within five years of the settlement or payment of the tax, and the claim for refund had to be based upon averment and proof that it had been held, since the payment of the tax, by a court of competent jurisdiction. The court found that the second condition had not been met and thus, the taxpayer was absolutely barred to the right to the refund.


OUTCOME: The decrees that dismissed the actions in mandamus were affirmed at the cost of the respective taxpayers.

 

474 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Genkinger v. New Castle


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 547; 84 A.2d 303; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 505


Argued September 26, 1951  
November 15, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 130, March T., 1951, from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County, March T., 1950, in Equity, No. 6, in case of Jacob F. Genkinger v. City of New Castle and The Officers' and Employees' Retirement Board and Edith M. Cleaveland, Cordelia Jones and J. Harold Wood. Decree affirmed.

Bill in equity. Before BRAHAM, P.J. and LAMOREE, J.

Adjudication filed finding for plaintiffs; exceptions by intervening defendants dismissed and final decree entered awarding injunctive relief. Intervening defendants appealed.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed at cost of the City of New Castle.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city, retirement board, and individuals, challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lawrence County (Pennsylvania), which declared null and void an ordinance of the city that set up a retirement system for its officers and employees and granted an injunction to prevent payments thereunder as requested by plaintiff citizen in his taxpayer's bill.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted an ordinance establishing a retirement system for its officers and employees pursuant to the Act of May 23, 1945 (Act), 1945 Pa. Laws 903, allowing pensions for current officers and employees and those who were later elected. The ordinance covered all the councilmen and would permit 4 of the 5 to retire on a pension at the end of their terms. On appeal, the court affirmed. Certain sections of the ordinance were void because they were based on a part of the Act that already had been declared void. One section of the ordinance was passed for the benefit of just one councilman. Another section allowed employees of privately funded city agencies to join the retirement system, and it deprived future councils of the right of amendment. The ordinance went far beyond the authorization of the Act and in some respects conflicted with it, it was fundamentally contrary to the public policy of Pennsylvania and to the purpose of a retirement or pension system, and it directly benefited four of the five councilmen in a manner and to a degree unauthorized by the legislation. The entire ordinance was void because the valid and the invalid sections were too inseparably interwoven.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which ruled in favor of the citizen in his taxpayer's bill against the city, declared null and void the ordinance establishing the retirement system, and enjoined payments thereunder.

Commonwealth ex rel. Maurer v. O'Neill


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 369; 83 A.2d 382; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 482


Argued January 2, 1951  
September 24, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 79, Jan. T., 1951, from order of the Court of Common Pleas No. 7 of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1949, No. 4170, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, ex rel. John H. Maurer, District Attorney of Philadelphia County, to use of George Braden, et al. v. Joseph P. O'Neill, et al. Order reversed; reargument refused October 30, 1951.

Quo warranto. Before OLIVER, P.J.

Adjudication filed finding for defendants; exceptions to adjudication dismissed and final decree entered dismissing complaint. Plaintiff appealed.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed and the appointments of the named defendants to the positions of captains of the Fire Bureau of the City of Philadelphia are set aside and declared null and void. Costs to be paid by defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant non-veteran challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas No. 7 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed a quo warranto complaint requesting promotion of the non-veteran. Appellee veterans also were parties in the case, which was brought in reliance upon the Veterans Preference Act of May 22, 1945, § 3, P.L. 837, as amended (Pa.).


OVERVIEW: Several fire fighters took an examination in order to become eligible for promotion to the rank of captain in the municipal fire bureau. Ten points were added to the examination scores of fire fighters who were military veterans. The non-veteran who was not promoted brought the quo warranto action claiming that § 3 was unconstitutional The common pleas court dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court reversed the order and invalidated the veterans' appointments. The court noted that under Pennsylvania common law, some reasonable relation was required to exist between the basis of a veterans preference and the object to be obtained. Public policy and constitutional restrictions prohibited unrestrained preference or preference credit based on factors not representative of true value. The court then held that the grant of 10 percentage points to veterans on promotion examinations, under § 3, constituted unreasonable and unconstitutional class legislation. The terms, "appointment," and "promotion" were not interchangeable, for purposes of the award of veterans preference. Interpreting them as synonymous violated the Statutory Construction Act of May 28, 1937, P.L. 1019.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and declared the veterans' appointments null and void.

Altieri v. Allentown Officers' & Employees' Retirement Board


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 176; 81 A.2d 884; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 460


Argued April 9, 1951  
June 27, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 137 and 140, Jan. T., 1951, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Sept. T., 1950, No. 229, in case of Alexander Altieri et al. v. Allentown Officers' & Employees' Retirement Board et al. Judgment at No. 137 reversed; appeal at No. 140 dismissed.

Mandamus.

Defendants' preliminary objections overruled and writ directed to issue, except as to plaintiffs Altieri and Hiller, as to whom writ dismissed without prejudice, opinion by HENNINGER, P.J. Plaintiff Altieri and defendants appealed.



DISPOSITION: The judgment under review at No. 137 is reversed and here entered for the defendant; the question raised at No. 140 has thus become moot and the appeal is therefore dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city retirement board appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which ruled that several of plaintiff former city employees were entitled to retirement benefits from the city.


OVERVIEW: Several former city employees filed suit against the city retirement board alleging they were entitled by the Retirement Act (Act), 53 P.S. §§ 12198-4371 to 4384, to receive retirement benefits following several years of service and involuntary termination. The trial court ruled that four of the employees over 60 years old were entitled to retirement benefits and that two younger employees were not. A younger employee and the retirement board sought review. On appeal, the court noted that the Act contained no age limitation at which employees were to retire when their services had been dispensed with involuntarily. The court held that a fixed age for the commencement of retirement was a requisite of the retirement system. The court determined that retirement acts were based on the theory of an adjusted compensation for time and service, payable in the future, provided the employee served the required length of time and reached the required age. The court held that regardless of age, the provision of the Act relied upon by the city employees was not enforceable as to city employees who were involuntarily terminated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that ruled that several city employees were entitled to retirement benefits from the city retirement board.

Miller v. Beaver Falls


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 189; 82 A.2d 34; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 463


Argued May 21, 1951  
June 27, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 110, March T., 1951, from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County, June T., 1950, in Equity, No. 8, in case of James N. Miller et ux. v. City of Beaver Falls. Judgment reversed; reargument refused August 1, 1951.

Bill in equity. Before SOHN, J.

Decree entered dismissing plaintiffs' bill. Plaintiffs appealed.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; costs to be paid by appellee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Beaver County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed the owners' action against defendant city challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance adopting a general plan for parks and playgrounds. The owners claimed that the ordinance was void as an unconstitutional encumbrance on their property.


OVERVIEW: The property owners, who intended to build houses on the land at issue, claimed that City of Beaver Falls, Pa., Ordinance No. 960 created a cloud upon their title. The lower court found that the establishment of a park and playground on the property, as intended by the Ordinance, was desirable and necessary to the development of the city. The court reversed the judgment dismissing the owners' action. Although the court agreed that parks were desirable, legislative acts were still subordinate to constitutional protections. Both the Pennsylvania and United States Constitutions required just compensation to be made where private property was taken for public use. While the plotting of a street did not constitute a taking, the court refused to extend this principle to the planning of parks and playgrounds. The city's action in plotting the owners' property was in effect an unconstitutional taking of their property. The court thus declared City of Beaver Falls, Pa., Ordinance No. 960 to be unconstitutional and void.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment dismissing the property owners' action against the city.

 

478 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Falk & Co. v. South Texas Cotton Oil Co.


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 199; 82 A.2d 27; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 464


Argued March 28, 1951  
June 27, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 91, March T., 1951, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1950, No. 132, in case of Falk & Company v. South Texas Cotton Oil Company and The Farmers Deposit National Bank of Pittsburgh. Order reversed.

Proceeding upon petition of defendant in foreign attachment proceeding and rule to show cause why service of process should not be set aside and writ of attachment quashed.

Order entered discharging rule, before THOMPSON, SMART and ADMAS, JJ., opinion by ADAMS, J. Defendant appealed.



DISPOSITION: Where it clearly appears that the proceeding is fundamentally illegal and void or where the facts clearly "put plaintiff completely out of court, or either of the statutory props required to sustain the foreign attachment is missing", it seems unfair and a waste of time to the Court and of time and money to the defendant and to the garnishee and totally unnecessary to require or permit further proceedings or to deny a writ to quash. In the present case the facts clearly demonstrate that the property attached was not the property of the defendant, and therefore the service of the sheriff on the garnishee should have been set aside and the [***2] writ of foreign attachment quashed. The Supreme Court has the power under the Act of May 20, 1891, P.L. 101, § 2, 12 P.S. § 1164, to enter such judgment, order or decree as may seem to us proper and just. We shall therefore enter the following order: The order of the court below is reversed, the rule to show cause is made absolute, the service of the writ of foreign attachment is set aside and the writ of foreign attachment is hereby quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foreign corporation sought review of a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted a writ of foreign attachment in favor of appellee domestic corporation. The writ of foreign attachment allowed the domestic corporation to attach the proceeds of certain drafts drawn on the domestic corporation by the foreign corporation.


OVERVIEW: The domestic corporation initiated a proceeding of foreign attachment in assumpsit against the foreign corporation and summoned a particular bank as garnishee. The trial court ordered the service of the writ on the garnishee and attached the proceeds of certain drafts drawn on the domestic corporation by the foreign corporation as the property of the foreign corporation. The foreign corporation filed an appeal. On review, the court reversed the order of the trial court and set aside and quashed the writ of foreign attachment. In reaching its decision, the court held that quashing was appropriate, because the property attached was not the property of the foreign corporation. The court held that the drafts, as well as their proceeds, were prima facie the property of the garnishee bank, as opposed to the foreign corporation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and set aside and quashed the writ of foreign attachment, holding that the property attached was not the property of the foreign corporation at the time of attachment.

 

479 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Williamson Estate


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


368 Pa. 343; 82 A.2d 49; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 479


Argued November 16, 1950; reargued May 24, 1951  
June 27, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 235 and 253, Jan. T., 1950, from decrees of Orphans' Court of Philadelphia County. Oct. T., 1930, No. 3498, in Estate of Ella Williamson, Deceased. Decrees affirmed; reargument refused September 26, 1951.

Same case below: 70 D. & C. 230.

Audit of account of trustee. Before KLEIN, J.

Esceptions to adjudication by trustee dismissed and final decree entered, before SINKLER, P.J., KLEIN, BOLGER, ladner, hunter/ and BOLAND, JJ., opinion by HUNTER, J., concurring opinion by LADNER, J., joined in by BOLGER, J. Trustee appealed.



DISPOSITION: Appeals dismissed; decrees affirmed at the cost of appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporate fiduciary of the relator estate challenged the judgment of the Orphans' Court of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that denied its request for increased compensation and interim commissions holding that it was bound by the long line of cases establishing principles of fiduciary compensation.


OVERVIEW: The corporate fiduciary of the decedent's trust sought increased compensation and interim commissions by inducing a revision of the compensation of trustees. The trial court rejected the attempt, finding that it was bound by cases establishing principles of fiduciary compensation. The court affirmed, holding that the principles fixing compensation applied equally to individuals and corporations and had been firmly established over 150 years. The court held that if conditions demanded revision, such radical change had to be made by the legislature and only after an investigation by a legislative committee concerning the situation of individual and corporate fiduciaries. The court noted that even if it was disposed to overrule the line of cases, the instant case was not an appropriate vehicle for such action. The court explained that the corporate fiduciary's acceptance of the trust in 1930 under the law as it then existed, accompanied by payment in full, vested the rights of the life tenant and remaindermen, and the imposition of additional compensation under a retroactive interpretation of the Fiduciary Act of 1945 would have been unconstitutional under the Fourteenth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying the corporate fiduciary's request for increased compensation and interim commissions and holding that it was bound by the long line of cases establishing principles of fiduciary compensation was affirmed.

Lukens v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


367 Pa. 608; 80 A.2d 765; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 428


Argued April 11, 1951  
May 23, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 87, Jan. T., 1951, from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, March T., 1950, No. 528, in case of James W. Lukens et ux. v. Zoning Board of Adjustment of Ridley Township, Delaware County. Judgment reversed.

Mandamus.

Plaintiffs' motion for judgment on the pleadings granted, opinion by ERVIN, P.J. Defendant appealed.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed at appellees' costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a township's Zoning Board of Adjustment (Board), appealed from the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), in plaintiff landowners' mandamus proceeding, granting the owners' motion for judgment on the pleadings and ordering the Board to hold a hearing on the owners' application for a special exception or variance to reclassify or redesignate certain portions of their property.


OVERVIEW: The Board ruled that it had no jurisdiction over the owners' petition and refused to consider it or hold a hearing on it. The owners filed a complaint in mandamus to compel the Board to consider it. The lower court sustained the owners' motion for judgment on the pleadings and directed the Board to hold a public hearing on their petition. The court reversed, agreeing with the Board that it lacked jurisdiction under the facts of the case. It noted that the owners' application was an original petition and not an appeal to the Board, and that there was no specific averment of unnecessary hardship or other statutory conditions required for a variance. The Board refused to consider the petition on two grounds: (1) that the petition was not an appeal; and (2) because the petition was really an application for a re-zoning. The court agreed, holding that the Board's only authority was statutory, and that the statute, being in derogation of property rights, had to be strictly construed and pursued. The statute gave the Board no original jurisdiction. In addition, the court found that the petition was improperly seeking a rezoning under the guise of a variance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The court held that it was unnecessary to decide whether or when mandamus would lie.

Quinn v. Pershing


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


367 Pa. 426; 80 A.2d 712; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 405


Submitted April 17, 1951  
May 21, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 179, March T., 1950, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Forest County, Sept. T., 1948, No. 19, in case of Bernard G. Quinn et al., on behalf of United Leather Workers International Union etc. v. George O. Pershing, International Fur & Leather Workers Union of the U.S. and Canada etc. Order affirmed.

Trespass for libel.

Order entered dismissing preliminary objections filed by defendant International Union, opinion by WADE, P.J. Defendant appealed.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. Costs to abide the event.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant union congress appealed an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Forest County (Pennsylvania), which overruled the union congress' objections to personal jurisdiction in a libel action filed by plaintiff leather workers union. The union congress contended that Pa. R. Civ. P. 2157 violated the privileges and immunities clause of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, and due process.


OVERVIEW: The leather workers union accused the union congress of libeling it during a representation election. The leather workers union filed this action and served the union congress by personally delivering the summons and complaint to an officer of the union congress. In affirming the order, the court held that this form of service, which was authorized by Rule 2157, comported with the privileges and immunities cause of the federal constitution and the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions. The court noted that the officer was the person in charge of the place where the union congress regularly conducted business. The court also held that because the rule allowed for this manner of service on both Pennsylvania residents and nonresidents, it did not offend the privileges and immunities clause. Due process was also satisfied by service on the union congress' officer, who was its agent in fact.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that overruled the union congress' objection to the service of process on its officer.

 

482 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Bryant


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


367 Pa. 135; 79 A.2d 193; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 358


Argued November 20, 1950  
March 21, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 286, Jan. T., 1950, from judgment and sentence of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County, Jan. Sessions, 1949, No. 410, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Edward J. Bryant. Judgment and sentence affirmed.

Indictment charging defendant with murder. Before ALESSANDRONI, J.

Verdict of guilty with penalty fixed at death and judgment of sentence entered thereon. Defendant appealed.



DISPOSITION: Judgment and sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that convicted him of murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant made incriminating statements to interrogating police officers, while in custody after his arrest, without having been advised of his right to counsel or of his privilege against self-incrimination. Although defendant did not claim that the statements were the product of any coercion or inducement practiced upon him, he argued that it was a violation of his due process rights, both under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9, to interrogate him because he had neither been provided with counsel, waived his right to counsel, nor been advised of his right to remain silent. The court disagreed and affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence. The court opined that the admission into evidence of the incriminating statements that defendant made to the police violated neither the federal nor the state constitution. The court reasoned that due process did not impose upon an arresting officer, or anyone else, the duty to inform an accused of his right to counsel from the moment of his arrest. The court explained that the criterion for adjudging the admissibility of an extrajudicially obtained confession was its testimonial trustworthiness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence of the trial court.

 

483 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Boyertown Burial Casket Co. v. Commonwealth


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


366 Pa. 574; 79 A.2d 449; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 332


Argued January 4, 1951  
March 19, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 9 and 10, May T., 1951, from orders of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Commonwealth Docket, 1948, Nos. 139 and 140, in case of Boyertown Burial Casket Co., v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. Orders affirmed.

Appeals by employer from determinations by Bureau of Unemployment Compensation of contribution rates.

The facts are stated in the opinion, by SMITH, J., of the court below, as follows:

These are appeals by the Boyertown Burial Casket Co. from determinations by the Bureau of Employment and Unemployment Compensation of the Department of Labor and Industry of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, (hereinafter called the "Bureau"), of appellant's unemployment compensation contribution rates for the years 1947 and 1948. The appeals were taken pursuant to the provisions of section 301 (h) of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law (Act of December 5, 1936, P.L. (1937) 2897, as amended by the Act of June 10, 1947, P.L. 498). It was agreed that both appeals should be heard and disposed of at the same time.

We accept as our findings the facts set forth in the stipulation of counsel filed for each of the appeals in question. Those necessary [***2] to an understanding of this opinion are as follows:

The Boyertown Burial Casket Co. (hereinafter called the "Company"), is an employer subject to the provisions of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law. Prior to the year 1947, the Company had been assigned the lowest possible rate of unemployment compensation contributions permitted under the experience rating provisions of the law.

On April 30, 1946, the Company had in its employ over three hundred employees in its Boyertown and East Greenville plants. On the date last mentioned the Upholsterers' International Union of America, a labor organization, called a strike at these two plants. Thereupon, the great majority of the Company's employees went out on a strike which lasted until October 28, 1946. A substantial number of the striking employees filed claims for unemployment compensation. These claims were allowed by the Bureau by its determination of June 26, 1946. 1 


1 Benefit rights of the Company's striking employees were determined under the provisions of section 402 (d) of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law as amended by section 9 of the Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145, then in force, which section provided as follows: "Section 402. Ineligibility for Compensation. -- An employee shall be ineligible for compensation for any week --... "(d) In which his unemployment is due to a voluntary suspension of work resulting from an industrial dispute, at the... premises at which he... was last employed: Provided, That this disqualification shall apply only to any week of unemployment which, in whole or in part, includes any part of the period beginning with the day on which such suspension occurs and ending with (i) the last day of the fourth calendar week immediately following the calendar week in which such suspension occurs, or (ii) the day on which such suspension was terminated, whichever is the earlier".

 [***3] From this initial determination by the Bureau, the Company duly appealed to the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review. The referee to whom the appeal was referred on August 7, 1946, the thereafter the Board of Review on February 10, 1947, affirmed the determination of the Bureau. Thereunder 279 of the Company's striking employees received compensation for each of the twenty statutory weeks from June 2, 1946 to October 26, 1946.

The Company having exhausted its remedies before the Board on March 14, 1947 duly appealed to the Superior Court. In paragraph 11 of its petition to the Superior Court for writ of certiorari (Stipulation of Facts, Exhibit "B", p. 137a), the Company averred: "The effect of the decision and order of said Board of Review, dated February 10, 1947 is to authorize the payments to these 279 claimants of a series of weekly benefits from June 1, 1946 to October 27, 1946, all of which will be charged against the individual employment record of your Petitioner and will thus place in jeopardy during each calendar quarter of 1947 and three subsequent years the present contribution rate of one per cent (1%) per annum now paid by your Petitioner. The [***4] Department of Labor and Industry as of January 1, 1945, for the years 1945 and 1946, assigned to your Petitioner a contribution rate of one per cent (1%) by reason of its good individual employment record during preceding years, which rating may have to be rerated upward under the law, and rules and regulations thereunder, in the event that these striking employees are deemed legally entitled to benefit payments. An increase in the contribution rate from 1% to 2.7% of the annual payroll of your Petitioner would probably cost your Petitioner fifteen thousand dollars ($1,5000), per year for three (3) years." Also in said petition, in the assignment of error and in the brief filed in the Superior Court the Company contended that, under the Act, no unemployment compensation payments could be made to those voluntarily not working by reason of a labor dispute and, further, that if the Act was so construed as to provide for such benefits, it was unconstitutional for the same reasons, inter alia, as averred in the instant appeals.

The Superior Court in Boyertown Burial Casket Company v. Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, 162 Pa. Superior Ct. 98, affirmed [***5] the Bureau's original determination of benefit eligibility on the part of the Company's striking employees. The Company then filed its petition for an allocatur to the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania. This petition was denied by that tribunal on April 22, 1948.

On or about April 1, 1947, the Bureau notified the Company that its contribution rate for the year 1947 would be 2.5%. On or about April 1, 1948, the Bureau notified the Company that its contribution rate for the year 1948 would be 2%. 2 In thus computing the Company's contribution rate for each of the two years in question the Bureau charged against its experience the benefit payments made as aforesaid to its striking employees. If such charges had not been made by the Bureau the Company's contribution rate would have been 1% for the first quarter of 1947 and 0.5% for the last three quarters of said year and for the year 1948.


2 By virtue of the provisions of the Act of June 10, 1947, P.L. 498, and the Act of July 9, 1947, P.L. 1469, the Company's contribution rate, as determined by the Bureau, was reduced to 1.5% for the second, third and fourth quarters of 1947 and 1% for the year 1948.

Applications [***6] for review and redetermination of the aforesaid contribution rates were filed by the Company with the Bureau on April 24, 1947 and April 26, 1948, respectively. Notice of the Bureau's denial of the Company's several applications was given to it on July 12, 1948, from which denials the instant appeals were duly taken to this Court.

Does a valid payment of unemployment compensation benefits made pursuant to the provisions of section 402 (d) of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law, as amended by section 9 of the Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145, to employees whose unemployment was due to their voluntary suspension of work in 1946 resulting from an industrial dispute at the establishment at which they were last employed, create a valid charge against the account of the base year employer of such employees in the computation of the employer's contribution rates for the years 1947 and 1948?

The Company in the instant appeals takes no issue with the allowance of unemployment compensation benefits awarded and paid to its striking employees. The decision of the Superior Court in Boyertown Burial Casket Company v. Unemployment Compensation Board of Review, 162 Pa. Superior Ct. 98, [***7] is conclusive of this question. See also section 509 of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law as amended by section 5 of the Act of April 23, 1942 (43 PS § 829) and Commonwealth v. Lentz, 353 Pa. 98. The Company, however, in these appeals does except to the inclusion in the computation of its "employer's experience" of the charges resulting from the payment of unemployment benefits to its striking employees on the ground that the Act in question, as amended by section 5 of the Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145 and in force in 1946, did not permit or require such benefit payments to affect an employer's rate of contribution and that to hold otherwise would render the Act unconstitutional and in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States and section 9 of Article I, sections 3 and 7 of Article III, and section 1 of Article IX of the Constitution of Pennsylvania. The Bureau on the other hand contends that the validity of the payment of benefits having been determined under the benefit appeal provisions of the Unemployment Compensation Law then in force (Article V as amended by section 11 of the Act of 1945, P.L. 1145),  [***8] the validity of the charges to the employer's account arising as the result thereof cannot be challenged in an appeal to this Court under section 301 of the law. 3 


3 Neither the 1947 Amendment to section 402(d) of the Act (P.L. 1186) whereunder payment of unemployment compensation in industrial dispute cases was abolished nor the 1949 Amendment to section 4 (y) thereof (P.L. 1854) excluding from the term "Wages of a Compensated Employe" unemployment benefits paid to strikers apply to the instant appeals.

Section 301 (k) of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law, as amended by section 5 of the Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145, provided: "Each employer shall be given notice of the filing of applications for benefits by his former employes as provided in section five hundred one. Notice having been properly given as provided in such section, no employer shall have standing, in any proceeding involving his rate of contribution, to contest the chargeability to his account of any wages of a compensated employe on the grounds that he was not given sufficient or adequate notice or opportunity to be heard." The position of the Company in effect is that, while in a [***9] proceeding under section 301 of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law the substantive rights of its employees to unemployment benefits cannot be litigated, nevertheless, under section 301 (k) supra, the validity of the charges against it arising from such payments can be questioned. With this position we cannot agree and we can find nothing in the Act to support it.

Prior to the Act of May 26, 1943, P.L. 639, the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law imposed a fixed contribution of 2.7% upon all employers subject to the provisions of that law. The Act of 1943 introduced the so-called "experience rating" provisions into the law establishing a system whereby certain employers who qualified could have their annual contribution rate fixed at a lower percentage. However, this Act, as well as subsequent amendments, retained a basic rate of 2.7%. 4 Nevertheless, the total amount of unemployment compensation contributions which an employer might be called upon to pay to any state is not measured exclusively by the provisions of the state law to which he may be subject. The Federal Unemployment Tax Act (26 U.S.C.A. 1600 et seq.) imposes [***10] a tax of 3% of payrolls upon all employers of eight or more subject to its provisions. Against the tax so imposed employers may take a credit up to 90% thereof of amounts paid by them into a state employment fund, provided the provisions of such state law meet the standards prescribed by the Federal Act. If the state law does not meet such standards, then the employer receives no credit for amounts paid into a state fund and is liable to the Federal Government for the full amount of tax imposed by the Federal Unemployment Tax Act. Included in the 90% credit is an additional credit hereinafter referred to.


4 Act of May 26, 1943, P.L. 639, sec. 301 (a) (4); Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145, sec. 301 (a) (3); sec. 301 (b); Act of June 10, 1947, P.L. 498, sec. 301 (A) (B).

The Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law meets the standards prescribed by the Federal Act. It creates what is known as a "pooled fund" system: Sturdevant Unemployment Compensation Case, 158 Pa. Superior Ct. 548, 564. With respect to allowable credits for sums paid by an employer under such system, section 1602 (a) of the Internal Revenue Code (26 U.S.C.A. 1602 [***11] (2)) provides: "(a) State standards. A taxpayer shall be allowed an aditional credit under section 1601 (b) with respect to any reduced rate of contributions permitted by a State law, only if the Federal Security Administrator finds that under such law -- "(1) No reduced rate of contributions to a pooled fund..., is permitted to a person (or group of persons) having individuals in his (or their) employ except on the basis of his (or their) experience with respect to unemployment or other factors bearing a direct relation to unemployment risk during not less than the three consecutive years immediately preceding the computation date;"

For the purpose of measuring an employer's "experience with respect to unemployment or other factors bearing a direct relationship to unemployment risk", the Pennsylvania Legislature adopted what is known as the "benefit wage ratio" system. The operation of such a system is briefly described in 55 Yale Law Journal 230, as follows: "When a worker is paid unemployment benefits each of his former employers is charged with the amount of wages he paid the worker during the worker's base period. The wages so charged are known as 'benefit [***12] wages' or 'beneficiary wages.' A ratio between all such wages charged against an employer during a three-year period and his total payroll during the same years is established which is known as the 'employer's experience factor'. Rates are not assigned directly in accordance with this index. Each employer's experience factor is first correlated with a state experience factor representing a ratio of all benefits paid in the state during the same three-year period to the benefit wages charged to all employers during that period. Under a schedule of rates which is provided, each employer's rate approximates the product of his experience factor multiplied by the state experience factor." These ratios, with respect to the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law, may be represented by the following formula:

Wages of a Compensated Employee 5 --

Re-employment Credit / Total Wages=Employer's Experience 6 

Total Net Compensation Paid / Total Wages of All Compensated=State Experience 7 

Employees 5 


5 Defined by section 4 (y), as amended by Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145.


6 Defined by section 4 (k), as amended by Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145 and Act of June 10, 19478 P.L. 498.


7 Defined by section 4 (s), as amended by Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145 and Act of June 10, 1947, P.L. 498.

 [***13] A "Compensated Employe" is one to whom unemployment compensation benefits have been paid in an amount equal to three times his weekly benefit rate in any one benefit year. 8 Benefits which an employee has received and to which he was found not to be entitled, are not included in the computation of such amount.


8 Section 4 (e), as amended by Act of May 29, 1945, P.L. 1145.

It thus appears that the "Wages of a compensated Employe" are an indispensable part of the formula provided in the Unemployment Compensation Law for the computation of the "Employer's Experience Factor" and the "State Experience Factor". Manifestly, unless a valid payment of benefits to an employe results in a valid charge to the employer's account, the formula will cease to be a measurement of the employer's "experience with respect to unemployment or other factors bearing a direct relation to unemployment risk". It necessarily follows, therefore, that a payment of benefits to the Company's striking employees have been determined to be valid under the provisions of Article V of the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law, as amended, the Law requires that a corresponding charge against [***14] the employer's account must also be made. In fact, as appears from the stipulation of counsel, the Company so construed the Law in its appeal and argument before the Superior Court from the allowance of unemployment benefits to its striking employees. Further, the action of the Legislature in 1947 in abolishing payment of unemployment benefits in industrial disputes and its action in 1949 in excluding from the term "Wages of a Compensated Employe" unemployment benefits paid to strikers would indicate that the Legislature so interpreted the law: See Statutory Construction Act of 1937, P.L. 1019, section 51 (46 PS 551.)

In Dawkins Unemployment Compensation Case, 358 Pa. 224, the late Chief Justice MAXEY speaking for the Supreme Court after setting forth the intendments of the Unemployment Compensation Act and holding that the Commonwealth is justified in the exercise of its police power in requiring certain employers to put aside unemployment reserves to be used for the benefit of persons unemployed through no fault of their own said, page 234: "These intendments make it clear that those within the protection of the Act are 'unemployed workers'...."

 [***15] Section 4 (u) of the 1945 amendment to the Act in force in 1946 provided that "an individual shall be deemed unemployed with respect to any week during which he performs no services and with respect to which no remuneration is paid or payable to him,...." And since the Superior Court has heretofore found that the Company's striking employees were "unemployed workers" within the special provisions of Section 4.2 (d) of the 1945 amendment, we find that thereunder unemployment reserves are required to be set aside by the Company to reflect the payment made to such "unemployed workers". 9 


9 If there is any conflict between the special provisions of section 402 (d) of the Act in force in 1946 allowing benefits to strikers after a penalty period and the general provisions of section 3, (43 PS 752), the title to which is "Declaration of public policy", stating that "involuntary unemployment" is the evil which the law is designed to remedy and that the general welfare and the public good require provision for the payment of benefits to persons "unemployed through no fault of their own", the special provisions of section 402 (d) take precedence over the general provisions of section 3. See Statutory Construction Act of 1937, P.L. 1019, Sections 63 and 64 (46 PS 563, 564).To the same effect see Bonomo Unemployment Compensation Case, 161 Pa. Superior Ct. 622.

 [***16] In view of the foregoing, as we construe the law in force in 1946, unemployment compensation benefits legally paid to employees engaged in an industrial dispute were required to be included by the Bureau in the determination of the employer's contribution rate. To hold otherwise would, in our opinion, defeat the entire intent and purpose of the "experience rating" provisions of the law as it then existed. We, further, find that the action of the Bureau in fixing the Company's contribution rates for the years 1947 and 1948, here appealed from, did not involve an erroneous construction of the statute as contended by the appellant.

Does the construction we have put upon the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law violate the Federal or State Constitutions? We think not. In the Company's appeal to the Superior Court from the aforesaid determination of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review allowing unemployment benefits to its striking employees, the Company raised, interalia, and argued the same constitutional questions as are raised in the instant appeals. See Stipulation of Facts, Exhibit "C", Assignments of Error Nos. 9, 10, 13, 14, 15, 21, 22, and Exhibit [***17] "D" attached thereto. It is true that the precise question before the Superior Court was as to the validity of the allowance of such benefit payments and not the question as to whether these benefit payments could be charged against the Company in the determination of its contribution rates for the years 1947 and 1948. However, the Company in support of its position before the Superior Court contended that, if the benefit payments in question were legal, its taxes or contributions to the fund under "experience rating" would necessarily be increased, thereby rendering the Act, or the decision of the Board, unconstitutional for the reasons as in the instant appeal set forth. Judge ARNOLD, speaking for the Superior Court, overruled all of these contentions upon the authority of a long line of cases cited in his opinion. 10 Further, after stating that "experience rating" under the amendment of 1943 P.L. 639, "was a reward and not a penalty" and that the policy of paying compensation when there was a voluntary suspension of work due to a labor dispute had been in effect for six years prior to said amendment, Judge ARNOLD said (162 Pa. Superior Ct. 98, 104): "Since [***18] we hold that it was constitutional prior to 1943, before 'experience rating' and when the tax was 2.7%, to provide unemployment compensation in the industrial dispute cases, -- it remained equally constitutional after the statute was amended to provide for the reward or adjustment of the employer's contribution under the formula of 'experience rating'. The adoption of the plan of 'experience rating' does not amend, modify or limit the benefit sections of the statute: Sturdevant Unemployment Compensation Case, 158 Pa. Superior Ct. 548, 549, 45 A. 2d 898. Therefore, if it be unconstitutional to have 'experience rating' affected by the payment of compensation in the industrial dispute cases, the 'experience rating' provisions must fall, and in this event, by the Act of 1943, supra, and all subsequent amendments, the intervenor's taxes were and are fixed at 2.7%...."


10 Lawrence Baking Co v. Michigan Unemployment Compensation Commission, (Mich.) 13 N.W. 2d 260; Tennessee Enamel Mfg. Co. v. Hake, Commissioner, (Tenn.) 194 S.W. 2d 468; W.H.H. Chamberlin, Inc. v. Andrews, Industrial Commissioner, et al., (N.Y.) 2 N.E. 2d 22; Fidelity-Philadelphia Trust Company et al. v. Hines, 337 Pa. 48, 10 A. 2d 553; Commonwealth v. Perkins, 342 Pa. 529, 21 A. 2d 45, affirmed by the United States Supreme Court, 314 U.S. 586; Department of Labor and Industry v. New Enterprise Rural Electric Cooperative, Inc., 352 Pa. 413, 43 A. 2d 90; Carmichael v. Southern Coal & Coke Company, 301 U.S. 494; Charles C. Steward Machine Company v. Davis, 301 U.S. 548. See also annotations in 109 A.L.R. 1346, and 118 A.L.R. 1220.

 [***19] In our opinion the above quoted conclusion reached by Judge ARNOLD, even if it could be held to be dicta insofar as the instant appeals are concerned, as contended by the Company, is fully justified by the overwhelming weight of the authorities cited by the Court and is here controlling. Again as stated in 48 Am. Jur., Social Security, Unemployment Compensation, etc., Section 4, pp. 516-7: "The state acts (providing for unemployment benefits) have been held to be a valid exercise of the taxing power of the states. It is immaterial that the state statute refers to payments under the act as 'contributions'; it will be held to be an exertion of the taxing power of the state when its constitutionality is in question and when it appears that the payment is required as a contribution to the public benefit. On the other hand, the taxing features of the acts have been held to be merely incidental to the paramount object of the acts to relieve against the distress of unemployment, and the legislation has been upheld as a valid exercise of the police power. The state is vested with power to impose taxes in general upon employers to alleviate unemployment, and the authority of [***20] the state is in no wise impaired by reason of blending the imposition of a tax with the relief of unemployment....

"The taxing provisions of the Federal Act do not deprive either employers or employees of their property without due process of law under the Fifth Amendment, nor do similar provisions in the state statutes deprive either of due process or of the equal protection of the law under the Fourteenth Amendment. Neither do they classify unfairly, although they do not apply to employers of fewer than a specified number of employees, and require employers with no unemployment to contribute to an unemployment fund, and exempt certain classes of employers and employees from their benefits and burdens...."

And in section 5 thereof, pp. 517-8: "Nor is an act, by reason of provisions for payment of benefits to... those out on strike,..., so arbitrary or unreasonable as to deprive employers of their property without due process of law, or of the equal protection of the law. It is likewise held that expenditures for the payment of unemployment benefits are for a valid public purpose although they are to be made only after a specified 'waiting period', and for not more [***21] than a certain amount, and for a limited period...."

Further, as apposite to the questions now under consideration, it was said in Lawrence Baking Co. v. Michigan Unemployment Compensation Commission, (Mich.) 13 N.W.2d 260, 265: "Plaintiff's argument is based upon the premise that the payment of compensation to employees on strike is a penalty upon the employer, because its rate of contribution to the unemployment fund will thereby be increased. The public purpose of the unemployment compensation law is to alleviate the distress of unemployment, and the payment of benefits is not conditioned upon the merits of the labor dispute causing unemployment. Likewise, the required contribution of the employer to the unemployment compensation fund is not determined upon the basis of the merits of the dispute. The increase in the amount of the employer's contribution to the fund because of its experience record of payments to employees is not in any sense a penalty. By the unemployment compensation act, the legislature provided a method of determining the employer's contribution to the compensation fund, and it did not see fit to base the amount of such contribution [***22] upon the merits of a labor dispute or upon the right or wrongdoing of the employer in connection with such dispute. Such legislative enactment is presumed to be constitutional unless the contrary clearly appears...."

It thus appears from the foregoing that the payment of the tax by the employers is required as a contribution to the public benefit to relieve against the distress of unemployment and as such is a valid exercise of the police power and not a taking of private property for private purposes; that the payment of unemployment benefits to those out on strike does not deprive the employers of their property without due process of law or of the equal protection of the law; that the Pennsylvania Unemployment Compensation Law is not a special or local Act "regulating labor, trade, mining and manufacturing"; and that the classifications made by the Act are valid and proper. Therefore, we find that the said Act as we have heretofore construed it does not violate either the Fourteenth Amendment to the Constitution of the United States or section 9 of Article I, section 7 of Article III, or section 1 of Article IX of the Pennsylvania Constitution.

No extended discussion [***23] is required in considering the Company's remaining constitutional objection that the statute as here construed violates section 3 of Article III of the Pennsylvania Constitution providing that "no bill, except general appropriation bills, shall be passed containing more than one subject, which shall be clearly expressed in its title".

The Company's sole contention on this phase of the case is that the above constitutional provision is violated because "no one from reading the title of the Act would be on notice that the legislature intended to compel an employer to make payments through the unemployment compensation fund to such of his employees as voluntarily refrained from working under the circumstances of this case". With this contention we cannot agree.

It is well settled by a multitude of cases that the title need not be an index nor a synopsis of the contents of the Act. On the contrary, all that is required is that the title shall contain words sufficient to cause one having a reasonably inquiring state of mind to examine the Act in order to determine whether or not he may be affected by it and that, unless a substantive matter entirely disconnected with the [***24] named legislation is included therein, the Act does not fall within the constitutional inhibition. 11 


11 Commonwealth ex rel. Schnader v. Liveright, 308 Pa. 35; Retirement Board of Allegheny County v. McGovern, 316 Pa. 161; Turco Paint & Varnish Company v. Kalodner, 320 Pa. 421; Commonwealth v. Stofchek, 322 Pa. 513; Kelley v. Earle, 325 Pa. 337; Poor District Case (No. 1), 329 Pa. 390; Annenberg v. Roberts, 333 Pa. 203; Hadley's Case, 336 Pa. 100; Equitable Loan Society, Inc. v. Bell, 339 Pa. 449; Bethlehem School District Appeal, 351 Pa. 433; Commonwealth v. American Gas Company, 352 Pa. 113. See also Stephano Brothers v. Secretary of Revenue, 54 Dauphin County Reports 157, 160-3.

As said by the Supreme Court in Commonwealth v. American Gas Company, 352 Pa. 113, 118-9. "In Commonwealth v. Stofchek, 322 Pa. 513, 185 A. 840, Kelley v. Earle, 352 Pa. 337, 190 A. 140, and Gumpert's Estate, 343 Pa. 405, 23 A. 2d 479, we had [***25] occasion to review this constitutional provision and to assert the principles governing its application. As stated in Gumpert's Estate, at page 407, by Mr. Justice HORACE STERN: 'It is unnecessary to elaborate upon the oft-repeated principle that all the Constitution requires is that the title should put persons of a reasonably inquiring state of mind on notice of the general subject matter of the act. The incidental provisions of the statute need not be enumerated or indexed in the title if they are germane to the legislation as a whole. "Unless a substantive matter, entirely disconnected with the named legislation, is included within the folds of the bill, the act should not be declared as a violation of the Constitution by reason of its title offending Section 3 of Article III."'".

The title of the Act insofar as here material, provides: "An Act establishing a system of unemployment compensation...; requiring employers to keep records and make reports, and certain employers to pay contributions based on payrolls to provide moneys for the payment of compensation to certain unemployed persons; providing procedure and administrative details for the determination, payment [***26] and collection of such contributions and the payment of such compensation;...."

In our opinion, this title clearly contained words sufficient to cause every employer to examine the Act to determine whether or not he was affected by it. Thereunder, every employer was put on notice that certain of them "were required to pay contributions based on payrolls to provide moneys for the payment of compensation to certain unemployed persons." And since we have theretofore held that the Company's striking employees were "unemployed persons" within the provisions of the Act in force in 1946, under the authorities hereinbefore cited, nothing more was required to be set forth in the title.

We, therefore, find that there is no merit in any of the Company's contentions. Both appeals must be dismissed.

Employer appealed.



DISPOSITION: The respective orders appealed from are affirmed on the opinion of Judge SMITH for the court below.

Orders affirmed.

Eagen v. Smith


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


366 Pa. 501; 78 A.2d 801; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 320


Argued January 5, 1951  
February 26, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal, No. 30, May T., 1951, from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Commonwealth Docket, 1950, No. 334, in case of Michael J. Eagen v. Gene D. Smith, Secretary of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al. Order affirmed.

Mandamus.

Order entered refusing writ, opinion by WOODSIDE, J. Petitioner appealed.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) refusing his petition for a writ of mandamus to compel appellee the Secretary of the Commonwealth to certify that he was lawfully elected a justice of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania at the general election.


OVERVIEW: Three members of the Supreme Court untimely died in the first half of 1950. These vacancies occurred more than 3 months before the November 7th election, but only the first of them preceded the primary election held May 16, by more than 10 weeks. Appellant contended that under Pa. Const. art. V, § 25 all vacancies occurring more than three months prior to the next general elected must be filled at that election. Appellant further contended that Pa. Const. art. V, § 16 prescribed the voting method for filling vacancies on the Supreme Court where more than one exist and that as to two of the vacancies here in question that section was satisfied. Appellant concluded that since he received the second highest number of votes at the November 7 election, he should be declared elected to the Supreme Court to fill the vacancy caused by the Chief Justice's death.. The court held neither constitutional section supplied a procedure for conducting an election, or how the electorate should be notified of the offices to be filled. The court determined it was clear that legislation was required to render the provisions of the Constitution relief upon by appellant effective.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Pittsburgh Coal Co. v. School Dist.


The Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


366 Pa. 489; 78 A.2d 253; 1951 Pa. LEXIS 318


Argued October 6, 1950  
January 2, 1951 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, No. 160, March T., 1950, and Nos. 5 and 6, March T., 1951, from judgments of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1949, No. 1591 and Oct. T., 1949, No. 2695 and April T., 1950, No. 11, in cases of Pittsburgh Coal Company v. School District of Forward Township, Same v. School District of Snowden Township, Same v. School District of Elizabeth Township. Judgments affirmed; reargument refused January 29, 1951.

Assumpsit.

Plaintiff's preliminary objections to defendants' answers in nature of demurrer overruled and judgment entered for defendants, before THOMPSON, WEISS and O'BRIEN, JJ., opinion by WEISS, J. Plaintiff appealed.



DISPOSITION: Each of the judgments appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company filed separate actions of assumpsit to recover taxes paid erroneously and inadvertently to defendants, three school districts. The taxes were imposed by due resolution of the respective districts. The Court of Common Pleas for Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) entered judgment in favor of the districts. The company sought review.


OVERVIEW: When case law held that taxing resolutions similar to those adopted by the districts were invalid, the company stopped making the tax payments and filed suit for refunds. The court held that (1) the express terms of the Act of May 21, 1943, P.L. 349, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 5566b, made it essential to an action to recover taxes that the taxes had been paid erroneously or inadvertently; (2) the company knew and understood what it was paying; (3) although the districts' resolutions were legally ineffective, the company's payments were intentional and deliberate; (4) the Act of June 25, 1947, No. 481, P.L. 1145, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 52, § 2015.1-2015.8, provided a specific right of appeal to taxpayers that was available to but not used by the company; and (5) as a result, the common law action of assumpsit was not proper for the recovery of taxes. The company did not have standing to question the payments that had been made to the districts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments.
