DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which denied their petition for witness immunity under the Immunity Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, §§ 640.1 and 640.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendant township supervisors were charged with extortion and prohibitive acts by a public official. The prosecution sought immunity for two witnesses. The court below found that the Immunity Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, §§ 640.1 and 640.2, under the circumstances did not empower the granting of immunity to these witnesses. The prosecution appealed the decision of the trial court. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court because it found that immunity from prosecution was initiated as a weapon against organized crime and racketeering and was to be strictly limited to that purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision because the court held that the prosecution was not entitled to immunity for two witnesses because the case did not involve organized crime or racketeering.

Commonwealth v. Walak


No. 133, April T., 1974


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


228 Pa. Super. 404; 323 A.2d 886; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1602


April 10, 1974, Argued  
June 21, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, April T., 1973, Nos. 262, 262a, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Roger Walak.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his drug conviction entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania) following a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's counsel filed a bill of particulars asking specifically for the names and addresses of any agents or any participants to the alleged criminal action. Prior to the selection of the jury on the date of trial, defendant's counsel renewed his request through oral motion asking that the prosecution furnish the whereabouts of a paid informant, or have the informant present during the trial. Defendant's motion was denied as being untimely. The prosecution did not call the informant as a witness. During the trial, defendant learned of the probable whereabouts of the informant from a narcotics agent. No request for a continuance was made. Defendant was convicted of drug charges. Defendant appealed his conviction. Defendant argued that the lower court erred in denying his application for the whereabouts of the informant. The prosecution's failure to provide this information to defendant was error. However, the court found that this error was harmless because defendant received the information during the trial and did not ask for a continuance. The prosecution did not committee the Kurtz error because that ruling was not applicable to this case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's drug conviction because the lower court's refusal to compel the prosecution to make the whereabouts of an informant, an eyewitness, known to the defense at the opening of the trial constituted harmless error and the prosecution did not commit the Kurtz error.

Commonwealth v. Brown


No. 64, Oct. T., 1974


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


229 Pa. Super. 156; 323 A.2d 345; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2169

DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor. Appellant contended that the trial court improperly instructed the jury on the presumptions of fact and innocence regarding intoxication.

The court also found that the trial court properly instructed the jury on the presumption of fact regarding intoxication and the presumption of innocence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which convicted appellant of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquor. The court found that the trial court's instruction, when taken as a whole, properly instructed the jury on the presumption of fact regarding intoxication and the presumption of innocence.

Commonwealth v. Quarles


No. 976, Oct. T., 1973


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


229 Pa. Super. 363; 324 A.2d 452; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2202


June 12, 1973, Argued  
June 21, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County, No. 2400 of 1972, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. John W. Quarles.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth sought review from the order of Court of Common Pleas of Bucks County (Pennsylvania), which suppressed the results of a breathalyzer test intended to be used against appellee driver. Appellant asserted that the implied consent law did not require a legal arrest before a breathalyzer test could be requested and that appellee's implied consent provided a constitutional basis for the seizure of appellee and the breath test.


OVERVIEW: Appellee driver was arrested by police after being involved in a vehicle accident. Appellee was then taken several miles away to a police station where a breathalyzer test was administered. An arrest warrant was issued after a summons was issued and preliminary hearing was held. A motion to suppress the results of the breathalyzer test was heard and granted and appellant Commonwealth sought review. Appellant argued that the results of the breath test should not have been suppressed due to the implied consent law and a variety of other related theories. On review, the court found that the implied consent law did not require a legal arrest before a breathalyzer test could be requested. Under the facts, the court ruled that appellee's implied consent provided a constitutional basis for the seizure of appellee and the breath test. But, the court ruled that a test that can be administered without transporting a person could be conducted if the requesting officer had reasonable grounds to believe a person was driving while intoxicated. Further, except in exceptional circumstances, a lawful arrest was required in order to transport a person. The decision was affirmed accordingly.


OUTCOME: The decision to suppress the results of the breathalyzer test was proper and that decision was affirmed because appellee driver could have been tested at the scene, but he could not without his actual consent have been transported several miles to a police station for testing, unless he was under legal arrest. It is to be noted that no exceptional circumstances existed in this case.

Commonwealth v. Young


No. 652, Oct. T., 1973; No. 1439, Oct. T., 1973


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


229 Pa. Super. 470; 325 A.2d 315; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2214


March 13, 1973, and June 12, 1973, Argued; September 10, 1973, Submitted  
June 21, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name and Counsel of this Case have been Corrected February 3, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Sept. T., 1970, No. 77, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Norman Young.

Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, No. 1175 of 1971, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. John Urenovich.



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed, convictions vacated, and appellants discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), and the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) entered judgments convicting appellants of flag desecration in violation of the Pennsylvania Flag Desecration Act, 18 P.S. § 4211 (Act). Appellants sought review of the judgments contending that the Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought review of judgments from the trial court convicting them of flag desecration in violation of the Pennsylvania Flag Desecration Act, 18 P.S. § 4211 (Act) for casting contempt upon the flag. The court reversed and vacated the convictions. The court held that the Act was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. The court found that the United States Supreme Court had found a substantially similar act from another state unconstitutionally void for vagueness because it did not define with substantial specificity what constituted forbidden treatment of the flag. As such, the court held that it was compelled to rule that the Act was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments and vacated the convictions. The court held that the state act prohibiting the casting of contempt upon the flag of the United States was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. The act failed to specify what constituted forbidden treatment of the flag.

Commonwealth v. Allen


No. 242, April T., 1973


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


227 Pa. Super. 157; 324 A.2d 437; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2038


November 12, 1973, Argued  
April 3, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County, Mar. T., 1972, No. 80, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. George Allen.



DISPOSITION: Order in arrest of judgment reversed, and case remanded for consideration of appellee's motion for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Mercer County (Pennsylvania) that granted an order in arrest of judgment after the jury had returned a verdict of guilty against appellee for receiving stolen goods and conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was found guilty of receiving stolen goods and conspiracy. However, an order in arrest of judgment was granted. On appeal, the order was reversed. The court held that the jury could have properly inferred from appellant's possession of the stolen check and the identification cards belonging to the victim that he knew they were in fact stolen. In a trial for receiving stolen goods, an inference of the requisite guilty knowledge could be drawn from the fact that appellant had the recently stolen goods in his possession. The possession and the attempt to negotiate the checks occurred less than twenty-four hours after their theft, therefore the inference was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that granted appellant's motion for an order in arrest of judgment, holding that the inference of guilty knowledge was permissible under the facts was virtually compelled. The court ruled that the jury could have inferred from appellant's possession of the stolen check and the identification cards belonging to the victim that he knew they were in fact stolen.

 

555 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Beam


Nos. 273 and 274, Oct. T., 1973


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


227 Pa. Super. 293; 324 A.2d 549; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2059


September 18, 1973, Argued  
April 3, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgments of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, March T., 1972, Nos. 262 and 263, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Alfred J. Beam.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgments of sentence by the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) for disorderly conduct, resisting arrest, and assault and battery. Defendant asserted that all the convictions should be dismissed because the disorderly conduct ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and because the state failed to bring all the charges in one proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shouted at and threatened a police officer with bodily harm and was arrested for and convicted of disorderly conduct in violation of a city ordinance. Defendant was then directed to a nearby police car, suddenly struck the police officer, and was convicted of resisting arrest and assault and battery. Defendant asserted that the disorderly conduct ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and that his arrest for its violation was unlawful. The court held that the vagueness of an ordinance did not give defendant the right to violently resist an arrest for conduct that an officer observed and reasonably believed to be in violation of the law. Defendant asserted that the charges should have been dismissed because a recent case required the state to bring them in one proceeding, as the charges arose from the same incident or transaction. The court held that this rule was not to be applied retroactively because the case was not decided to apply to traditional double jeopardy guarantees of successive prosecutions for the same offense and would not promote the purposes of the rule's adoption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments of sentence of defendant for disorderly conduct, resisting arrest, and assault and battery, because a vague disorderly conduct ordinance did not give defendant the right to resist arrest and the state was not required to bring all the charges in one proceeding.

 

556 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Knaub


No. 57, Mar. T., 1974


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


226 Pa. Super. 526; 323 A.2d 366; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2120


March 11, 1974, Submitted  
April 3, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of York County, May T., 1971, No. 307, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Eugene L. Knaub.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania) that convicted and sentenced appellant for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of an intoxicating liquor. Appellant's contentions centered on the lower court's refusal to suppress a breathalyzer test.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury on the charge of operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of an intoxicating liquor. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed, holding that appellant lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute establishing the breathalyzer test because he submitted to the test and therefore was not prejudiced by the statute. Given the police officer's testimony that appellant consented to the test after Miranda warnings, the trial court had discretion to disbelieve appellant's testimony that that he was coerced into taking the breathalyzer test because the officer warned that if he did not he would lose his license for six months. Finally, 75 P.S. § 624.1(G) did not require the officer to inform appellant that he had a right to a second test by a physician of his choosing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for operating a motor vehicle while under the influence of an intoxicating liquor, holding that the lower court did not err in refusing to suppress a breathalyzer test.

 

557 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Horace


No. 1732, Oct. T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


226 Pa. Super. 531; 323 A.2d 895; 1974 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2122


December 10, 1973, Argued  
April 3, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Nov. T., 1971, No. 1093, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Cleveland Horace.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that convicted and sentenced appellant for aggravated robbery. Appellant contended that the lower court improperly invoked Pa. R. Crim. P. 312 to exclude alibi testimony as part of his defense.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with aggravated robbery. Prior to trial, defense counsel gave untimely notice that he intended to call an alibi witness to show that appellant was not at or near the scene of the crime at the time of the alleged crime. The lower court rejected appellant's request and ordered him to proceed to trial without the alibi witness. Appellant testified in his own behalf, a guilty verdict was returned, and he was sentenced to one to three years' imprisonment. Appellant sought review. The court reversed and remanded, holding that Pa. R. Crim. P. 312 violated the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it required appellant to give timely notice of an alibi defense without giving him reciprocal discovery rights to be apprised of anti-alibi witnesses that the commonwealth would call to rebut appellants' alibi witnesses. Hence, the lower court's enforcement of Rule 312 required the grant of a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded appellant's conviction and sentence for aggravated robbery, holding that appellant was entitled to a new trial because enforcement of an alibi notice rule failed to provide reciprocal discovery. As such, appellant's constitutional right to due process was violated.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order and judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Nos. 72-0655 and 72-7529, in case of G.A.G. Corp., a Pennsylvania Corp. v. Herbert and Esther Auritt and R. Wheatcroft.



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant assignee sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which permitted appellee tax sale purchaser to take possession of the mortgaged premises, granted summary judgment in favor of appellee tax sale purchaser, declared the mortgage lien discharged, and removed the stay on eviction in appellant's mortgage foreclosure action against appellee tax sale purchaser.


OVERVIEW: Appellant assignee filed a mortgage foreclosure action against appellee tax sale purchaser. The trial court permitted appellee to take possession of the mortgaged premises, granted summary judgment in favor of appellee, declared the mortgage lien discharged, and removed the stay on eviction. The court affirmed the allowance of eviction on behalf of appellee and grant of summary judgment in favor of appellee because the appeals should not have been transferred to another court, the appeal should have been quashed, the trial court's ruling on the merits was correct. The court further found that the mortgage lien did not survive the tax sale and appellant was not entitled to rely upon its survival. 560 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Wiegand v. Wiegand


No. 251, April T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


226 Pa. Super. 278; 310 A.2d 426; 1973 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1356


April 11, 1973, Argued  
September 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Family Division, of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1967, No. 439, in case of Sara Wiegand v. Myron Paul Wiegand.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which directed him to pay counsel fees, costs, and expenses in a divorce action.


OVERVIEW: Appellee wife filed a complaint in divorce, a petition for alimony, and an initial petition for alimony pendente lite, counsel fees and expenses, which the trial court granted. After several hearings on additional petitions, the trial court entered an order, which required appellant to pay counsel fees and costs. The issue considered by the appellate court was whether the divorce code under Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 11, 46, upon which the trial court based its order, was constitutional in light of the Equality of Rights Amendment (ERA) to Pa. Const. art. I, § 27. Pa. Const. Stat. §§ 11, 46 provided certain rights and remedies to a wife who obtained a divorce. By contrast, the ERA provides that the equality of rights under the law shall not be denied or abridged in the commonwealth of Pennsylvania because of the sex of the individual. The court held that because §§ 11, 46 afforded rights exclusively to females, the rights of males in Pennsylvania were abridged by these sections solely because of their sex and, therefore, were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that awarded counsel fees and expenses to appellee wife in her divorce action against appellant husband and held that the statutory provisions under which the order was based violated the Equality of Rights Amendment to the Pennsylvania constitution, as they afforded exclusive rights and remedies to females.

Lovejoy v. Georgeff


No. 752, Oct. T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


224 Pa. Super. 206; 303 A.2d 501; 1973 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1883


December 4, 1972, Argued  
April 12, 1973, Decided

Nicholas Georgeff, administrator of the estate of Daniel Georgeff, deceased, and Nicholas Georgeff, individually, and Robert J. Georgeff.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant automobile passenger and passenger's father challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which granted a motion by appellee administrator of deceased driver's estate for a judgment on the pleadings in appellants' personal injury action against appellee.

567 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Sampson-Miller Associated Cos. v. Landmark Realty Co.


No. 221, April T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


224 Pa. Super. 25; 303 A.2d 43; 1973 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1849


November 17, 1972, Argued  
March 27, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, April T., 1972, No. 273, in case of Sampson-Miller Associated Companies, Inc. v. Landmark Realty Company.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) dismissing appellant's complaint and striking a mechanics' lien for work performed on vacant land.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation filed a mechanics' lien for work performed on vacant land owned by appellee realty company. The work furnished involved clearing, grubbing, excavating and grading the land; installation of storm sewers, sanitary sewers, paving and curving; and seeding. No buildings or other permanent structures were built on the land. The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing appellant's complaint and striking the mechanics' lien. The court concluded that a lien could not attach to land and that there had to be a building or part of a building or some type of structure upon which a lien could attach. The court found that the Mechanics' Lien Law of 1963, 49 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1101 et seq., provided protection for those who did preliminary work similar to that performed by appellant, but only if the work was connected to the erection, construction, alteration or repair of a permanent building or structure. The court was constrained by the plain words of the statute and by the expressions of the intention of the legislature, and as such, the court held that no lien could attach to land for work unconnected to the construction of a building.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing appellant corporation's complaint and striking a mechanics' lien for work performed on vacant land. The court concluded that the plain words of the statute and the expressions of the intention of the legislature made it clear that no lien could attach to land for work unconnected to the construction of a building.

569 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hartley


No. 912, Oct. T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


223 Pa. Super. 541; 302 A.2d 378; 1973 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2169


September 11, 1972, Argued  
March 27, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, No. 383 of 1971, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Donald Hartley.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which imposed sentence on its conviction of defendant for criminal libel, 18 P.S. § 4412, after the Pennsylvania Supreme Court decided that the statute under which defendant was convicted was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of criminal libel pursuant to 18 P.S. § 4412. Sentence was deferred pending a presentence investigation. Approximately three months later the trial court imposed a sentence of three hundred dollars plus costs of prosecution; sentence of imprisonment was suspended, and defendant was placed on one-year probation. In the interim, prior to defendant's sentencing, the Pennsylvania Supreme Court held that the criminal libel statute was unconstitutional. Defendant contended that he should be discharged because the effect of the supreme court decision, prior to his being sentenced, was to make defendant's acts no longer criminally proscribed. He argued that, because the case was before the court on direct appeal, the judgment of the sentence was not final, and the court could grant the relief requested. The court reversed the judgment of sentence, vacated the conviction, and discharged defendant. Because the supreme court decision was decided prior to sentence, and because the instant appeal came directly from the judgment of sentence in a timely fashion, the court was bound to reverse the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence, vacated the conviction, and discharged defendant. The judgment of sentence imposed was not final, so the court could grant the relief sought. Because the decision of the state supreme court, which rendered criminal libel unconstitutional was decided prior to imposition of sentence, and the direct appeal was in a timely fashion, the court was obligated to vacate and reverse the conviction.

Commonwealth v. Amick


Nos. 1275 and 1276, Oct. T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


223 Pa. Super. 237; 298 A.2d 905; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1085


December 6, 1972, Submitted  
December 11, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Blair County, Oct. T., 1968, No. 2 and Oct. T., 1969, No. 12, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Kenneth I. Amick.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and appellant discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County (Pennsylvania), which issued a judgment of sentence for the offense of possession of obscene matter with the intent to sell.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted for the offense of possession of obscene matter with the intent to sell. The allegedly obscene material was obtained in two searches of appellant's book store, made pursuant to general search warrants. Appellant's motion to suppress was denied. On appeal, the court reversed and ordered appellant discharged. The court held that the warrants issued and executed were in violation of appellant's rights under U.S. Const. amend IV. The warrants used to make the mass seizures were general in nature, lacking any pretext of particularity, and hence were constitutionally prohibited. Moreover, the warrants were issued and executed without a prior adversary proceeding to determine whether the materials to be seized were obscene, thus constituting a prior restraint on both appellant's and the public's right to freedom of expression.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence and ordered appellant discharged. The court held that the warrants issued and executed in the seizure of allegedly obscene materials from appellant's store were in violation of appellant's rights under the fourteenth amendment.

Commonwealth v. Fattizzo


No. 1492, Oct. T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


223 Pa. Super. 378; 299 A.2d 22; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1103


September 12, 1972, Argued  
December 11, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Feb. T., 1970, Nos. 907 and 908, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Alfred Mario Fattizzo.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth appealed from the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which dismissed indictments against defendant in a state gambling prosecution because defendant had testified before a federal grand jury and had been granted immunity pursuant to 18 U.S.C.S. § 2514.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for being a common gambler and for setting up and maintaining an illegal lottery. Prior to trial on these charges, defendant appeared before a federal grand jury investigating gambling and invoked his privilege against compulsory self-incrimination. He was granted immunity. Defendant then filed a motion for dismissal of the state indictments pending against him, which was granted, and the commonwealth appealed. The court affirmed. The court reasoned that 18 U.S.C.S. § 2514 contemplated both transactional and use immunity, that immunity from prosecution was intended to apply in state criminal proceedings, and that the statute's prohibition on the state prosecution was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court dismissing the indictments because defendant had been granted immunity to testify before a grand jury in a separate case.

573 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Mitek v. Ste-Mel Signs, Inc.


No. 763, Oct. T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 395; 294 A.2d 813; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1298


June 15, 1972, Argued  
September 19, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1964, No. 4928, in case of Stanley M. Mitek v. Ste-Mel Signs, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant salesman sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied the petition of appellant to proceed in forma pauperis in his appeal of an inadequate judgment granted him against appellee company by an arbiter.


OVERVIEW: Appellant salesman filed suit against appellee company for unpaid commissions. An arbiter found in favor of appellant. However, appellant deemed the award too low and wished to appeal to the trial court. Appellant petitioned to proceed in forma pauperis, which was denied by the trial court, finding that a prevailing party could not proceed in forma pauperis, relying on 5 P.S. § 72 (1836), which provided that the party against whom any award was made could petition that by reason of poverty he was unable to pay the costs of suit and the court if satisfied as to the truth of the averments could allow the appeal without prepayment of the costs. The trial court denied appellant's petition because he won the arbitration award. On review, the court reversed, holding that, independent of § 72, the petition should have been granted, because plaintiff would have had the right under the common law to appeal in forma pauperis without restriction as to whether or not the appeal was from an award against him or from an inadequate award in his favor.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, which denied the petition of appellant salesman to proceed in forma pauperis. The fact that appellant was the prevailing party in arbitration did not preclude proceeding in forma pauperis.

Commonwealth v. Tarabilda


No. 990, Oct. T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 237; 294 A.2d 830; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1268


June 12, 1972, Submitted  
September 15, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, July T., 1970, No. 1028, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. William Tarabilda.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of transferring a narcotic drug in violation of the Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. § 780-1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for transferring a narcotic drug in violation of the Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act (DDCA), 35 P.S. § 780-1 et seq. (1961). At the time of defendant's conviction, a narcotic drug was defined as any drug or other substance found and proclaimed by the U.S. Secretary of the Treasury to have an addiction-forming or addiction-sustaining liability pursuant to §2(g) of the DDCA, 35 P.S. § 780-2(g) (1961). On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the standards set forth in § 780.2(g) were sufficiently clear and definite to permit the classification of narcotic drugs by the Secretary. The court also found that the DDCA provided constitutionally sufficient warning to defendant and was neither vague nor uncertain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, finding that the standards set forth in the Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act were constitutionally sufficient and that the delegation of the authority to classify narcotic drugs by the legislature to the U.S. Secretary of the Treasury was valid.

 

575 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Kolodziejski v. Tancredi


No. 1715, Oct. T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 436; 295 A.2d 174; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1308


June 12, 1972, Argued  
September 15, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Family Court Division, of Philadelphia, Jan. T., 1969, Nos. 4115 and 4116, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Hedwig Francine Kolodziejski v. Pasquale Aeriules Tancredi.



DISPOSITION: Order dismissing proceedings reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which rescinded its earlier order dismissing defendant's criminal charges for fornication, bastardy, and neglect to support a child born out of wedlock.


OVERVIEW: Defendant denied paternity and was ordered held for trial on charges of fornication and bastardy in order to establish his liability for support of a minor child, in complainant mother's action for the same. Defendant sought dismissal of the criminal charges based in part on the statute of limitations under 18 P.S. § 4732. This was granted, then later set aside, as the lower court concluded that the statute of limitations had been tolled. The court disagreed, as there was no filed criminal complaint that tolled the statute, nor did any other part of the proceedings do the same. As the bills of indictment were not presented to the grand jury and defendant did not waive presentment, the bills did not toll the statute. The court held that the filing of the civil complaint did not toll the statute as it could not be construed as a criminal complaint, and a hearing on that complaint did not toll the statute as it was not considered a preliminary hearing. Because the court found that the statute was not tolled, further criminal proceedings against defendant were barred. The court thus reinstated the order dismissing all criminal proceedings against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reinstated the order dismissing all criminal proceedings against defendant. The court found that the statute of limitations was not tolled, and further criminal proceedings against defendant were barred. A criminal complaint had not been filed and the civil complaint that had been filed did not operate to toll the statute. A hearing on the civil complaint also did not toll the statute, as it was not considered a preliminary hearing.

Commonwealth v. Mason


Nos. 76 and 77, March T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 453; 295 A.2d 103; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1310


March 13, 1972, Argued  
September 15, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgments of sentences of Court of Common Pleas of Mifflin County, Nos. 21 and 22 of 1970, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Benjamin J. Mason.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence in criminal action No. 21 affirmed; in criminal action No. 22 reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Mifflin County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of malicious libel pursuant to 18 P.S. § 4412 and of violating the anonymous communications provision of 18 P.S. § 4414.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of maliciously libeling several public officials pursuant to 18 P.S. § 4412 and violating the anonymous communications provision of 18 P.S. § 4414. Defendant offered no testimony and there was no evidence that any of the statements he made were truthful. Defendant sought review of the trial court's decision alleging that the criminal libel legislation and Pa. Const. art. I, § 7 were of no force because they did not comport with federal constitutional guarantees concerning freedom of speech and freedom of the press. The court affirmed the decision in part because § 4412 prohibited negligent criminal libel convictions but did not prohibit convictions based on malicious and untruthful statements. Defendant also contended that the communications did not violate § 4414 because they were not anonymous, although unsigned as their authorship was publicly known, they were serially numbered, and were published in similar fashion for a long period of time. The court reversed defendant's conviction for violating § 4414 because the mere technical absence of a signature was not intended to be a violation of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence convicting defendant of malicious libel because defendant's statements about the public officials were malicious and untruthful. The court reversed defendant's judgment of sentence for violating the anonymous communications provision of the criminal libel legislation because the communications were not anonymous.

 

577 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Skufca


No. 500, April T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 506; 294 A.2d 787; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1318


April 10, 1972, Submitted  
September 15, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, April T., 1970, Nos. 276 and 277, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Luella Skufca.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which, following the death of two children, convicted her of involuntary manslaughter and negligence and abandonment of minor children.


OVERVIEW: Prior to leaving her apartment, appellant mother secured her two young children in a bedroom by latching the door. Thereafter, the apartment caught on fire and the children died from suffocation. Consequently, appellant was convicted of involuntary manslaughter and negligence and abandonment of minor children. On review, the court affirmed the convictions. The court noted that the tort definition of proximate cause in manslaughter cases could no longer be used. However, the court also noted that a defendant could not escape criminal responsibility for homicide where their violence produced a condition causing death, although the violence itself was not the immediate cause of death. The court ruled, in view of the facts, that proof of causation beyond a reasonable doubt was present in that appellant was negligent and reckless in locking the bedroom door in such a way that that not only was exit prevented but also entrance by another. The court ruled that the applicable statute was not unconstitutionally vague in that in the phrase "abandons the child in destitute circumstances" the word "destitute" was widely known and used in society.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for involuntary manslaughter and negligence and abandonment of minor children. The court found that while appellant's actions were a proximate cause of her children's deaths, appellant was still criminally liable for such deaths because of the negligent and reckless manner in which she locked the children in her bedroom. The court also ruled that the applicable statute was not unconstitutionally vague.

Commonwealth v. McFarland

579 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Clark


No. 105, March T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 1; 292 A.2d 488; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1227


March 13, 1972, Argued  
June 16, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Sept. T., 1964, No. 163, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Richard Harrison Clark.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the denial of his petition for writ of habeas corpus, entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), attacking the new sentence he received for his conviction of sodomy after the Barr-Walker Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, § 1166 (1951), was found unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of assault and solicitation to commit sodomy on a five-year-old boy under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4502, and was sentenced to a term of one day to life pursuant to the provisions of the Barr-Walker Act (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 1166. The Act containing § 1166 was later found unconstitutional. The trial court then vacated appellant's sentence and imposed in its stead a sentence of not less than five or more than 10 years. The trial court refused to grant appellant a writ of habeas corpus. The court vacated appellant's judgment of sentence and discharged him, holding that he had already served the maximum sentence of five years under § 4502. The trial court could not sentence appellant to double that sentence because appellant's victim did not meet the definition of an associate of appellant within the meaning of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 5110. Specifically, a child under seven years of age was conclusively presumed to have no capacity to commit a crime, and therefore could not be a principal, accomplice, or accessory thereto.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of sentence and discharged appellant, finding that he had already served his maximum sentence under the statute proscribing assault and solicitation to commit sodomy. Appellant could not be required to serve a sentence of twice the maximum statutory penalty because his victim, a five-year-old boy, could not be an associate of the crime under the statute.

581 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


International Equity Corp. v. Pepper & Tanner, Inc.


No. 316, Oct. T., 1972


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


222 Pa. Super. 118; 293 A.2d 108; 1972 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1246


March 23, 1972, Argued  
June 15, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Aug. T., 1970, No. 70-10546, in re International Equity Corporation v. Pepper and Tanner, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant buyer sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's petition to open a judgment confessed against appellant in appellee seller's action, under a cognovit provision in the parties' agreement, to recover the balance due on a note.


OVERVIEW: Appellant buyer purchased a line of men's toiletries from appellee seller. Appellant transferred a judgment note to appellee, which provided for installment payments and confession of judgment under the cognovit system upon default. Appellant transferred a portion of the line to another corporation; the transferee corporation agreed to pay the remaining balance on the note. The note became in default, and appellant paid a portion of the balance. No further payments were made, and appellee took judgment on the note. The trial court dismissed appellant's petition to open the judgment. Appellant argued that the Pennsylvania statutes permitting judgments by confession were unconstitutional in their entirety. The court affirmed the judgment without addressing the constitutionality of the statutes as a whole, holding that the cognovit statutes were constitutional to the facts of this case, as appellant was a huge business and no class was involved. Appellant could not seriously contend that its officers or counsel were not aware of the significance of the cognovit provision. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's petition to open the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, holding that the cognovit statutes were properly applied to appellee seller's action for confessed judgment, under a cognovit provision in the parties' agreement, because appellant buyer was engaged in a huge business and the case did not proceed as a class action; the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying appellant's petition to open the judgment.

585 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Riccardi


No. 869, Oct. T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


220 Pa. Super. 72; 283 A.2d 719; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1111


September 13, 1971, Argued  
November 11, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, April T., 1970, No. 129, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Anthony Riccardi.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which entered judgment convicting appellant of receiving stolen goods and violating the Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act. Appellant's pre-trial application to suppress evidence was dismissed, and appellant's post-trial motions in arrest of judgment and for a new trial were denied.


OVERVIEW: After obtaining a valid search warrant for appellant's residence, police enlisted the aid of appellant's building superintendent to gain entry into appellant's home. When appellant partially opened the door, police converged and forced their way inside, despite appellant's effort to close the door. The search revealed allegedly stolen baked goods and several packets of the drug methamphetamine hydrochloride. This evidence was introduced by the commonwealth at appellant's trial. Appellant was convicted of receiving stolen goods and violating the Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act. Appellant contended his convictions were improper because the physical evidence upon which both convictions were based was obtained as a result of an unconstitutional search and seizure. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial, holding the entry by the police violated the standards of U.S. Const. amend. IV because the police failed to adhere to the requirements of the knock and announce rule. The court held that, therefore, the evidence seized in the ensuing search was improperly admitted at appellant's trial.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment convicting appellant of receiving stolen goods and violating the Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act was reversed and remanded for a new trial. The lower court erred in admitting evidence seized by the police following their forcible entry into appellant's home because they did not give him time to voluntarily surrender the premises.

588 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Appeals of Munnell


No. 443, April T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


219 Pa. Super. 525; 281 A.2d 906; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1421


November 10, 1970, Argued  
September 21, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, April T., 1970, No. 9, in re appeal of Jeffrey C. Munnell; George R. Reetz, t/a Reetz Typewriter Shop; and Eger Motors, Inc. v. City of McKeesport.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, 33 individuals and 10 corporations, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania). The order affirmed the validity of a business privilege tax from an ordinance that was enacted pursuant to the taxing authority given to third-class cities by the Local Tax Enabling Act, 33 P.S. § 6901 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city, a third-class city, passed an ordinance providing for a business privilege tax. The ordinance was enacted pursuant to the taxing authority given to third-class cities by the Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 P.S. § 6901 et seq. The ordinance imposed a six-mill tax on the privilege of doing business in the city and was measured by the gross receipts of the business. Appellants, 33 individuals and 10 corporation, challenged the adoption of the tax. The lower court affirmed the validity of the tax. The superior court affirmed the lower court's order. The enabling act authorized appellee to impose a business privilege tax, the tax was uniform since the taxpayers did not meet their burden of proving that the taxing classifications were unreasonable, the taxing statute did not authorize an unlawful delegation of taxing authority, and the tax was not invalid under the enabling act. There was no need to consider whether the legislature intended double taxation since the superior court concluded that the business privilege tax did not duplicate either the earned income tax or the occupational privilege tax.


OUTCOME: The superior court affirmed a lower court's order that affirmed the validity of the business privilege tax in favor of appellee city, and against appellants, 33 individuals and 10 corporations. The tax was uniform where the taxpayers failed to prove unreasonable taxing classifications. An unlawful delegation of taxing authority did not exist in the taxing statute, and the tax was valid under the enabling act that authorized tax imposition.

 

589 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jacobs


No. 1309, Oct. T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


220 Pa. Super. 31; 279 A.2d 251; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1103


December 7, 1970, Argued  
April 15, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas, Family Division, of Philadelphia, March T., 1970, No. 4649, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Alfonso Jacobs.



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant putative father sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied his motions for a new trial and in arrest of judgment, and entered an order of support for the minor child.


OVERVIEW: Appellant putative father was summoned to appear before the trial court pursuant to the provisions of the Pennsylvania Civil Procedural Support Law, 62 P.S. § 2043.31 et seq., after a child's mother filed a complaint alleging appellant was the father of the child. Prior to trial, appellant and the district attorney agreed to try the matter civilly, by judge alone. The trial court denied the commonwealth's motion to nol pros. and appellant's motion to proceed civilly, on the basis that it did not have jurisdiction to try a contested paternity issue as a civil matter or to enter a support order without a prior determination in a criminal proceeding. Appellant was tried and found guilty of neglect to support a bastard. On appellant's request for review, the issue before the court was whether the alleged paternity of a putative father had to be determined through a criminal proceeding for fornication and bastardy and/or neglect to support a bastard, or whether the putative father could choose to have the issue decided as a civil matter. The court vacated the trial court judgment and determined that appellant would be allowed to have paternity determined in a civil proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court judgment that denied appellant's motions for a new trial and in arrest of judgment, and ordered him to pay child support. The court held that appellant would be allowed to have paternity determined in a civil nonjury proceeding, and that it was not necessary to try all putative fathers in criminal proceedings for fornication, bastardy, or neglect to support a bastard.

Commonwealth v. Macek


No. 143, April T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


218 Pa. Super. 124; 279 A.2d 772; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1626


November 13, 1970, Argued  
April 15, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1969, No. 129, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Goldie Mae Macek.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied her motion in arrest of judgment, wherein defendant claimed that because the jury did not find her guilty of prostitution, as claimed by the arresting police officer, a fortiori, there was no probable cause for her arrest on that charge and consequently the search of her purse was not incident to a lawful arrest.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of possession of marijuana, in violation of The Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act (the act), 35 P.S. § 780-1 et seq., but acquitted of prostitution and assignation. Defendant was arrested for prostitution and a search of her purse after arrest revealed marijuana. Defendant did not object to the mode of securing the marijuana, either by pretrial motion to suppress or by objection at trial. The trial court denied defendant's motion in arrest of judgment, wherein defendant claimed that because the jury did not find her guilty of prostitution, there was no probable cause to arrest. The court found that the ensuing search of defendant's purse was in incident to a lawful arrest. Defendant appealed the order. The court disagreed with defendant's contention that because the jury did not find her guilty of the charge on which she was arrested that her arrest was without probable cause. The court found that defendant waived her claim by not filing a pretrial motion or objecting at trial. The court found that there was nothing in the record entitling defendant to suppress the evidence. The court rejected defendant's contention that the act was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence finding defendant guilty of possession of marijuana but not guilty of prostitution and assignation. The court rejected defendant's contention that her acquittal established a lack of probable cause for her arrest. The court also held that defendant waived the issue by not raising it in a pretrial motion or as an objection at trial.

Commonwealth v. Saxon


No. 21, March T., 1971


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


219 Pa. Super. 64; 275 A.2d 876; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1336


September 14, 1970, Argued  
March 23, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of York County, Oct. T., 1968, No. 171, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Thaddeus Saxon.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal sought review from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of interference with law enforcement or firefighting personnel and endangering lives of such personnel during riotous or tumultuous conditions in a case brought by the commonwealth of Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal challenged his conviction of interference with law enforcement or firefighting personnel and endangering lives of such personnel during riotous or tumultuous conditions. Appellant contended that his conviction could not stand because PA. Penal Code § 402.1 (the Act) upon which the indictment and conviction were based was unconstitutional. Appellant also contended that the instructions to the jury did not limit the charges against him to a constitutional interpretation and application of the Act. The court held that the statute set out the act, which it intended to prohibit, that being, interference with policemen and firemen in the performance of their duties during riotous or tumultuous conditions. Therefore, the indictment and the charge sufficiently narrowed the application of the statute to a constitutional construction and that such construction cured any vagueness or overbroadness. The language of the Act was sufficiently definite to warn appellant of the nature and character of conduct it proscribed. The court found no violation of appellant's constitutional rights in the application of the statute to him. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of appellant criminal convicted of interference with law enforcement or firefighting personnel and endangering lives of such personnel during riotous or tumultuous conditions in a case brought by the commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The court held that the indictment and the charge sufficiently narrowed the application of the statute to a constitutional construction that cured any vagueness or overbroadness.

Commonwealth v. Dial


No. 153, April T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


218 Pa. Super. 248; 276 A.2d 314; 1971 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1660


November 12, 1970, Argued  
March 23, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgments of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Sept. T., 1968, No. 173, Oct. T., 1968, No. 235 and Nov. T., 1968, No. 303, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Charles Dial.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed at No. 173, Sept. T., 1968, and No. 235, Oct. T., 1968 and judgment of sentence reversed at No. 303, Nov. T., 1968.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's motion to suppress evidence seized in three different searches and seizures that was used to convict on three separate narcotic charges.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a known narcotic addict and dealer who was convicted and punished for with three counts of unlawful possession of narcotic drugs. Appellant challenged the constitutionality of the three searches and seizures and the admissibility of the narcotics seized. The court found that the warrant issued for a search of appellant's apartment was based on probable cause and properly executed. The warranted was issued based on information from an informant that was supported by police surveillance indicating that appellant was preparing narcotics in his apartment. Another search occurred incident to a lawful arrest, and the narcotics were discovered during a permissible weapons search. The third search took place pursuant to a warrant for a search appellant's person; however, the court held that the warrant was constitutionally defective because the affidavit for the warrant did not give the magistrate any facts or circumstances from which he could reasonably conclude that narcotics were on appellant's person. Because the state presented no other evidence to convict appellant for possession of the narcotics seized under this warrant, the charges were dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed two of appellant convictions on narcotics charges. The narcotics admitted into evidence to support the third charge were illegal seized pursuant to an illegal search warrant; therefore, the narcotics were inadmissible. The state presented no other evidence to support the conviction so the charges were dismissed.

Commonwealth v. Ray


No. 987, Oct. T., 1969, and No. 392, Oct. T., 1970


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


218 Pa. Super. 72; 272 A.2d 275; 1970 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1080


September 14, 1970, Argued  
December 10, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused December 24, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from judgments of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1968, No. 519, and Oct. T., 1969, No. 444, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Frederick Ray; Same v. Hosie Jeffcoat.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged judgments of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which dismissed prosecutions against appellee individuals for violations of Philadelphia, Pa., Code §§ 10-814, 10-818 and indictments for carrying a concealed weapon and carrying a firearm without a license in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4628 and its amendments, on the grounds that the ordinances and amendments were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee individuals were charged with firearm violations. The first one was tried for carrying a concealed weapon and unlawfully carrying a firearm without a license. He was also charged with the summary violation of the licensing provision of Philadelphia, Pa. Ordinance 10-814. The second individual was charged with the same indictable offenses and a violation of Ordinance 10-818, for carrying a firearm in public. The trial court directed verdicts of not guilty of the indictable offenses and held Ordinances 10-814 and 10-818 and the 1968 amendments to the Uniform Firearms Act , 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4628(e)(1), (2), unconstitutional. The state could not appeal the directed verdicts, but sought review of the constitutional determinations. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. It held that the challenged ordinances exceeded the authority of a home rule city because they attempted to extend the city's authority beyond its limit and impermissibly enlarged on and limited the acts of the state legislature. Section 4628(e)(1), (2), however, enjoyed a presumption of constitutionality as to the right to bear arms and was upheld.


OUTCOME: The trial court was affirmed in part and reversed in part by the court. It held that amendments to the firearms act were constitutional because they enjoyed a presumption that the legislature would not act in contravention of the constitutional right to bear arms. The city firearm ordinances were unconstitutional because they exceeded the authority of a home rule city to legislate beyond its borders and in conflict with state law.

 

596 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Arnold


No. 1006, October Term, 1969


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


215 Pa. Super. 444; 258 A.2d 885; 1969 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1140


September 10, 1969, Argued  
November 13, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Clearfield County, May T., 1969, No. 56, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Dan P. Arnold.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The commonwealth challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Clearfield County (Pennsylvania), which held that the statute mandating use of protective headgear by those riding on motorcycles was unconstitutional, thereby dismissing the charges against defendant.


OVERVIEW: The trial court did not take any testimony when it reached its conclusion that 75 P.S. § 625.1 (1968), the section of the Vehicle Code that mandated the use of protective headgear by any person operating or riding on a motorcycle, was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded to the trial court for proper proceedings to determine defendant's guilt or innocence. The court held that § 625.1 was constitutional as a reasonable exercise of Pennsylvania's police powers. The court held that the commonwealth could appeal because the issue was purely of law and not an acquittal. The court found that the regulation bore a reasonable and substantial relation to the attainment of public safety on the highways and that it met the requirements of constitutional validity. The court found that there was benefit flowing to the public because the statutory requirement protected the person riding on the motorcycle from loose stones on the highway kicked up by passing vehicles. The court found that a person riding on a motorcycle could be a menace to others on the highway if such objects injured him.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order below and remanded the case to the trial court to determine defendant's guilt or innocence because the statute mandating use of protective headgear on persons riding motorcycles was constitutional as a proper exercise of Pennsylvania's police powers.

In re Petty


No. 1008, Oct. T., 1969


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


216 Pa. Super. 55; 258 A.2d 874; 1969 Pa. Super. LEXIS 859


September 9, 1969, Argued  
November 12, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Cameron County, Sept. T., 1968, No. 24, and No. 78 of 1968, in matter of suspension of restaurant liquor license No. R-10946 and amusement permit No. AP-11611, issued to Douglas and Gretchen Petty, trading as The Cottage.



DISPOSITION: Order of court below reversed and order of board reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Cameron County (Pennsylvania), which modified appellant's order suspending the liquor license of appellee restaurant to provide for suspension for a period of 30 days, rather than 60 days as ordered by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board suspended the restaurant liquor license of appellee restaurant for a period of 60 days after appellant found that appellee sold liquor to visibly intoxicated persons, conducted its establishment in a noisy and/or disorderly manner, served liquor after hours, and permitted dancing after hours. The trial court reduced the suspension from 60 days to 30 days, ruling that the establishment was not conducted in a noisy and/or disorderly manner and that the proof concerning appellee's sales to visibly intoxicated persons fell short of sustaining the charge against it. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's order and reinstated appellant's 60 suspension. The court reiterated that the conduct of a licensed establishment in a noisy and/or disorderly manner is sufficient cause for suspension of the license and appellant had power and authority under 47 P.S. § 4-471 to enter an order of suspension on that ground. The court went on to find that the uncontradicted testimony of the enforcement officers regarding sales to visibly intoxicated persons was sufficient proof to sustain that charge against appellee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and reinstated the order of appellant Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board suspending the liquor license of appellee restaurant for a period of 60 days. The court reiterated that the conduct of a licensed establishment in a noisy and/or disorderly manner was sufficient cause for suspension of a license and held that appellant was within its power in ordering a 60 day suspension.

599 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Ford v. Hendrick


Nos. 757, 758, October Term, 1969


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


215 Pa. Super. 206; 257 A.2d 657; 1969 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1106


June 13, 1969, Argued  
September 11, 1969, Decided
